<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Paleocene–Eocene Thermal Maximum</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"PETM" redirects here. It is not to be confused with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PETN" title="PETN">PETN</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:65_Myr_Climate_Change.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="397" data-file-width="650" height="183" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/65_Myr_Climate_Change.png/300px-65_Myr_Climate_Change.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/65_Myr_Climate_Change.png/450px-65_Myr_Climate_Change.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/65_Myr_Climate_Change.png/600px-65_Myr_Climate_Change.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:65_Myr_Climate_Change.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Climate change during the last 65 million years as expressed by the oxygen isotope composition of benthic foraminifera. The Paleocene-Eocene Thermal Maximum (PETM) is characterized by a brief but prominent negative excursion, attributed to rapid warming. Note that the excursion is understated in this graph due to the smoothing of data.</div></div></div>
<p>The <b>Paleocene–Eocene Thermal Maximum</b> (<b>PETM</b>), alternatively <span class="nowrap">"<b>Eocene thermal maximum 1</b>"</span> (<b>ETM1</b>), and formerly known as the "<b>Initial Eocene</b>" or "<b><span class="nowrap">Late Paleocene Thermal Maximum</span></b>", with more than 8 °C warmer global average temperature than today.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="Not in next ref. (September 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> This climate event began at the time boundary of the <a href="/wiki/Paleogene" title="Paleogene">Paleogene</a>, between the <a href="/wiki/Paleocene" title="Paleocene">Paleocene</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eocene" title="Eocene">Eocene</a> geological <a href="/wiki/Epoch_(geology)" title="Epoch (geology)">epochs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Westerhold2008_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Westerhold2008-1">[1]</a></sup> The exact age and duration of the event is uncertain but it is estimated to have occurred around 55.5 million years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bowen2015_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bowen2015-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p><p>The associated period of massive carbon injection into the atmosphere has been estimated to have lasted no longer than 20,000 years. The entire warm period lasted for about 200,000 years. Global temperatures increased by 5–8 °C.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McInerney2011_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-McInerney2011-3">[3]</a></sup> The carbon dioxide was likely released in two pulses, the first lasting less than 2,000 years. Such a repeated carbon release is in line with current <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bowen2015_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bowen2015-2">[2]</a></sup> A main difference is that during the Paleocene–Eocene Thermal Maximum, the planet was essentially ice-free.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> However, the amount of released carbon, according to a recent study, suggest a modest 0.2 gigatonnes per year (at peaks 0.58 gigatonnes); humans today add about 10 gigatonnes per year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ArsAug17_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-ArsAug17-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>The onset of the Paleocene–Eocene Thermal Maximum has been linked to an initial 5 °C temperature rise and to extreme changes in Earth's <a href="/wiki/Carbon_cycle" title="Carbon cycle">carbon cycle</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McInerney2011_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-McInerney2011-3">[3]</a></sup> The period is marked by a prominent negative excursion in carbon <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stable_isotope" title="Stable isotope">stable isotope</a> (<a href="/wiki/%CE%9413C" title="Δ13C"><span class="texhtml">δ</span><sup>13</sup>C<sub></sub></a>) records from around the globe; more specifically, there was a large decrease in <sup>13</sup>C/<sup>12</sup>C ratio of marine and terrestrial <a href="/wiki/Carbonate" title="Carbonate">carbonates</a> and organic carbon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McInerney2011_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-McInerney2011-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kennett1991_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kennett1991-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Koch1992_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Koch1992-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p><p>Stratigraphic sections of rock from this period reveal numerous other changes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McInerney2011_3-3"><a href="#cite_note-McInerney2011-3">[3]</a></sup> Fossil records for many organisms show major turnovers. For example, in the marine realm, a mass extinction of <a href="/wiki/Benthos" title="Benthos">benthic</a> <a href="/wiki/Foraminifera" title="Foraminifera">foraminifera</a>, a global expansion of subtropical <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dinoflagellates" title="Dinoflagellates">dinoflagellates</a>, and an appearance of excursion, planktic foraminifera and calcareous nanofossils all occurred during the beginning stages of PETM. On land, modern <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammal</a> orders (including primates) suddenly appear in Europe and in North America. Sediment deposition changed significantly at many <a href="/wiki/Outcrop" title="Outcrop">outcrops</a> and in many drill cores spanning this time interval.
</p><p>At least since 1997, the Paleocene–Eocene Thermal Maximum has become a focal point of considerable <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geoscience" title="Geoscience">geoscience</a> research because it probably provides the best past analog by which to understand <a href="/wiki/Effects_of_global_warming" title="Effects of global warming">impacts of global climate warming</a> and of massive carbon input to the ocean and atmosphere, including <a href="/wiki/Ocean_acidification" title="Ocean acidification">ocean acidification</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dickens1_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dickens1-9">[9]</a></sup> Although it is now widely accepted that the PETM represents a "case study" for global warming and massive carbon input to Earth's surface,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McInerney2011_3-4"><a href="#cite_note-McInerney2011-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zeebe2009_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zeebe2009-10">[10]</a></sup> the cause, details and overall significance of the event remain perplexing.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Setting">Setting</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Paleocene%E2%80%93Eocene_Thermal_Maximum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Setting">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The configuration of oceans and continents was somewhat different during the early <a href="/wiki/Paleogene" title="Paleogene">Paleogene</a> relative to the present day. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Panama_Isthmus" title="Panama Isthmus">Panama Isthmus</a> did not yet connect <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a> and <a href="/wiki/South_America" title="South America">South America</a>, and this allowed direct low-latitude circulation between the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean">Pacific</a> and <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">Atlantic Oceans</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Drake_Passage" title="Drake Passage">Drake Passage</a>, which now separates <a href="/wiki/South_America" title="South America">South America</a> and <a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">Antarctica</a>, was closed, and this perhaps prevented thermal isolation of Antarctica. The <a href="/wiki/Arctic" title="Arctic">Arctic</a> was also more restricted. Although various proxies for past atmospheric CO<sub>2</sub> levels in the Eocene do not agree in absolute terms, all suggest that levels then were much higher than at present. In any case, there were no significant ice sheets during this time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zachos2008_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zachos2008-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p><p>Earth surface temperatures increased by about 6 °C from the late Paleocene through the early Eocene, culminating in the "Early Eocene Climatic Optimum" (EECO).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zachos2008_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-Zachos2008-13">[13]</a></sup> Superimposed on this long-term, gradual warming were at least two (and probably more) "hyperthermals". These can be defined as geologically brief (&lt;200,000 year) events characterized by rapid global warming, major changes in the environment, and massive carbon addition. Of these, the PETM was the most extreme and perhaps the first (at least within the <a href="/wiki/Cenozoic" title="Cenozoic">Cenozoic</a>). Another hyperthermal clearly occurred at approximately 53.7 Ma, and is now called <a href="/wiki/Eocene_Thermal_Maximum_2" title="Eocene Thermal Maximum 2">ETM-2</a> (also referred to as H-1, or the Elmo event). However, additional hyperthermals probably occurred at about 53.6 Ma (H-2), 53.3 (I-1), 53.2 (I-2) and 52.8 Ma (informally called K, X or ETM-3). The number, nomenclature, absolute ages, and relative global impact of the Eocene hyperthermals are the source of considerable current research. Whether they only occurred during the long-term warming, and whether they are causally related to apparently similar events in older intervals of the geological record (e.g. the <a href="/wiki/Toarcian_turnover" title="Toarcian turnover">Toarcian turnover</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Jurassic" title="Jurassic">Jurassic</a>) are open issues.
</p><p>Acidification of deep waters, and the later spreading from the North Atlantic can explain spatial variations in carbonate dissolution. Model simulations show acidic water accumulation in the deep North Atlantic at the onset of the event.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Evidence_for_global_warming">Evidence for global warming</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Paleocene%E2%80%93Eocene_Thermal_Maximum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Evidence for global warming">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Global_Cenozoic_Temperature-en.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="639" data-file-width="335" height="572" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Global_Cenozoic_Temperature-en.svg/300px-Global_Cenozoic_Temperature-en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Global_Cenozoic_Temperature-en.svg/450px-Global_Cenozoic_Temperature-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Global_Cenozoic_Temperature-en.svg/600px-Global_Cenozoic_Temperature-en.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Global_Cenozoic_Temperature-en.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A stacked record of temperatures and ice volume in the deep ocean through the Mesozoic and Cenozoic periods.<br/>LPTM— Paleocene-Eocene Thermal Maximum<br/>OAEs— Oceanic Anoxic Events<br/>MME— Mid-Maastrichtian Event</div></div></div>
<p>At the start of the PETM, average global temperatures increased by approximately 6 °C (11 °F) within about 20,000 years. This warming was superimposed on "long-term" <a href="/wiki/Climate_across_Cretaceous%E2%80%93Paleogene_boundary" title="Climate across Cretaceous–Paleogene boundary">early Paleogene warming</a>, and is based on several lines of evidence. There is a prominent (&gt;1<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Per_mil" title="Per mil">‰</a>) negative excursion in the <a href="/wiki/%CE%9418O" title="Δ18O"><span class="texhtml">δ</span><sup>18</sup>O</a> of foraminifera shells, both those made in surface and deep ocean water. Because there was a paucity of continental ice in the early Paleogene, the shift in <span class="texhtml">δ</span><sup>18</sup>O<sub></sub> very probably signifies a rise in ocean temperature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thomas1996_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas1996-15">[15]</a></sup>
The temperature rise is also supported by analyses of fossil assemblages, the Mg/Ca ratios of foraminifera, and the ratios of certain <a href="/wiki/Organic_compound" title="Organic compound">organic compounds</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/TEX86" title="TEX86">TEX<sub>86</sub></a>.
</p><p>Precise limits on the global temperature rise during the PETM and whether this varied significantly with latitude remain open issues. Oxygen isotope and Mg/Ca of carbonate shells precipitated in surface waters of the ocean are commonly used measurements for reconstructing past temperature; however, both paleotemperature proxies can be compromised at low latitude locations, because re-crystallization of carbonate on the seafloor renders lower values than when formed. On the other hand, these and other temperature proxies (e.g., TEX<sub>86</sub>) are impacted at high latitudes because of seasonality; that is, the “temperature recorder” is biased toward summer, and therefore higher values, when the production of carbonate and organic carbon occurred.
</p><p>Certainly, the central Arctic Ocean was ice-free before, during, and after the PETM. This can be ascertained from the composition of sediment cores recovered during the Arctic Coring Expedition (ACEX) at 87°N on <a href="/wiki/Lomonosov_Ridge" title="Lomonosov Ridge">Lomonosov Ridge</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moran2006_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Moran2006-16">[16]</a></sup> Moreover, temperatures increased during the PETM, as indicated by the brief presence of subtropical dinoflagellates,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> and a marked increase in TEX<sub>86</sub>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sluijs2006_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sluijs2006-18">[18]</a></sup> The latter record is intriguing, though, because it suggests a 6 °C (11 °F) rise from ~17 °C (63 °F) before the PETM to ~23 °C (73 °F) during the PETM. Assuming the TEX<sub>86</sub> record reflects summer temperatures, it still implies much warmer temperatures on the North Pole compared to the present day, but no significant latitudinal amplification relative to surrounding time.
</p><p>The above considerations are important because, in many global warming simulations, high latitude temperatures increase much more at the poles through an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ice-albedo_feedback" title="Ice-albedo feedback">ice-albedo feedback</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shellito2003_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shellito2003-19">[19]</a></sup> It may be the case, however, that during the PETM, this feedback was largely absent because of limited polar ice, so temperatures on the Equator and at the poles increased similarly.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Evidence_for_carbon_addition">Evidence for carbon addition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Paleocene%E2%80%93Eocene_Thermal_Maximum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Evidence for carbon addition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Clear evidence for massive addition of <sup>13</sup>C-depleted carbon at the onset of the PETM comes from two observations. First, a prominent negative excursion in the carbon isotope composition (<span class="texhtml">δ</span><sup>13</sup>C<sub></sub>) of carbon-bearing phases characterizes the PETM in numerous (&gt;130) widespread locations from a range of environments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McInerney2011_3-5"><a href="#cite_note-McInerney2011-3">[3]</a></sup> Second, carbonate dissolution marks the PETM in sections from the deep sea.
</p><p>The total mass of carbon injected to the ocean and atmosphere during the PETM remains the source of debate. In theory, it can be estimated from the magnitude of the negative carbon isotope excursion (CIE), the amount of carbonate dissolution on the seafloor, or ideally both.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dickens1_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dickens1-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zeebe2009_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-Zeebe2009-10">[10]</a></sup> However, the shift in the <span class="texhtml">δ</span><sup>13</sup>C<sub></sub> across the PETM depends on the location and the carbon-bearing phase analyzed. In some records of bulk carbonate, it is about 2‰ (per mil); in some records of terrestrial carbonate or organic matter it exceeds 6‰.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McInerney2011_3-6"><a href="#cite_note-McInerney2011-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Norris1999_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Norris1999-20">[20]</a></sup> 
Carbonate dissolution also varies throughout different ocean basins. It was extreme in parts of the north and central Atlantic Ocean, but far less pronounced in the Pacific Ocean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zeebe2009_10-2"><a href="#cite_note-Zeebe2009-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Panchuk2008_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Panchuk2008-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cui2011_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cui2011-22">[22]</a></sup>
With available information, estimates of the carbon addition range from about 2000 to 7000 gigatons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zeebe2009_10-3"><a href="#cite_note-Zeebe2009-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Panchuk2008_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-Panchuk2008-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cui2011_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cui2011-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Comparison_with_today.27s_climate_change"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Comparison_with_today's_climate_change">Comparison with today's climate change</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Paleocene%E2%80%93Eocene_Thermal_Maximum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Comparison with today's climate change">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Model simulations of peak carbon addition to the ocean–atmosphere system during the PETM give a probable range of 0.3–1.7 Pg C/yr, which is much slower than the currently observed rate of carbon emissions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> It has been suggested that today's methane emission regime from the ocean floor is potentially similar to that during the PETM.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Timing_of_carbon_addition_and_warming">Timing of carbon addition and warming</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Paleocene%E2%80%93Eocene_Thermal_Maximum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Timing of carbon addition and warming">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The timing of the PETM <span class="texhtml">δ</span><sup>13</sup>C<sub></sub> excursion is of considerable interest. This is because the total duration of the CIE, from the rapid drop in <span class="texhtml">δ</span><sup>13</sup>C<sub></sub> through the near recovery to initial conditions, relates to key parameters of our global carbon cycle, and because the onset provides insight to the source of <a href="/wiki/Carbon-13" title="Carbon-13"><sup>13</sup>C</a>-depleted CO<sub>2</sub>.
</p><p>The total duration of the CIE can be estimated in several ways. The iconic sediment interval for examining and dating the PETM is a core recovered in 1987 by the <a href="/wiki/Ocean_Drilling_Program" title="Ocean Drilling Program">Ocean Drilling Program</a> at Hole 690B at <a href="/wiki/Maud_Rise" title="Maud Rise">Maud Rise</a> in the South Atlantic Ocean. At this location, the PETM CIE, from start to end, spans about 2 m.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kennett1991_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kennett1991-7">[7]</a></sup> Long-term age constraints, through biostratigraphy and magnetostratigraphy, suggest an average Paleogene sedimentation rate of about 1.23 cm/1,000yrs. Assuming a constant sedimentation rate, the entire event, from onset though termination, was therefore estimated at about 200,000 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kennett1991_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-Kennett1991-7">[7]</a></sup> Subsequently, it was noted that the CIE spanned 10 or 11 subtle cycles in various sediment properties, such as Fe content. Assuming these cycles represent precession, a similar but slightly longer age was calculated by Rohl et al. 2000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rohl2000_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rohl2000-25">[25]</a></sup> A ~200,000 duration for the CIE is estimated from models of global carbon cycling.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dickens2000_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dickens2000-26">[26]</a></sup>
If a massive amount of <sup>13</sup>C-depleted CO<sub>2</sub> is rapidly injected into the modern ocean or atmosphere and projected into the future, a ~200,000 year CIE results because of slow flushing through quasi steady-state inputs (weathering and volcanism) and outputs (carbonate and organic) of carbon.
</p><p>The above approach can be performed at many sections containing the PETM. This has led to an intriguing result.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giusberti2007_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Giusberti2007-27">[27]</a></sup> At some locations (mostly deep-marine), sedimentation rates must have decreased across the PETM, presumably because of carbonate dissolution on the seafloor; at other locations (mostly shallow-marine), sedimentation rates must have increased across the PETM, presumably because of enhanced delivery of riverine material during the event.
</p><p>Age constraints at several deep-sea sites have been independently examined using <sup>3</sup>He contents, assuming the flux of this cosmogenic nuclide is roughly constant over short time periods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Farley2003_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Farley2003-28">[28]</a></sup> 
This approach also suggests a rapid onset for the PETM CIE (&lt;20,000 years). However, the <sup>3</sup>He records support a faster recovery to near initial conditions (&lt;100,000 years)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Farley2003_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-Farley2003-28">[28]</a></sup> than predicted by flushing via weathering inputs and carbonate and organic outputs.
</p><p>There is other evidence to suggest that warming predated the <span class="texhtml">δ</span><sup>13</sup>C<sub></sub> excursion by some 3,000 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sluijs2007_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sluijs2007-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Effects">Effects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Paleocene%E2%80%93Eocene_Thermal_Maximum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Effects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Weather">Weather</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Paleocene%E2%80%93Eocene_Thermal_Maximum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Weather">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The climate would also have become much wetter, with the increase in evaporation rates peaking in the tropics. <a href="/wiki/Deuterium" title="Deuterium">Deuterium</a> isotopes reveal that much more of this moisture was transported polewards than normal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pagani2006_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pagani2006-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ocean">Ocean</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Paleocene%E2%80%93Eocene_Thermal_Maximum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Ocean">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The amount of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Freshwater" title="Freshwater">freshwater</a> in the Arctic Ocean increased, in part due to northern hemisphere rainfall patterns, fueled by poleward storm track migrations under global warming conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pagani2006_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pagani2006-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Anoxia">Anoxia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Paleocene%E2%80%93Eocene_Thermal_Maximum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Anoxia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Anoxic_event" title="Anoxic event">Anoxic event</a></div>
<p>In parts of the oceans, especially the north Atlantic Ocean, <a href="/wiki/Bioturbation" title="Bioturbation">bioturbation</a> was absent. This may be due to <a href="/wiki/Anoxic_event" title="Anoxic event">bottom-water anoxia</a>, or by changing ocean circulation patterns changing the temperatures of the bottom water. However, many ocean basins remained bioturbated through the PETM.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zachos2005_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zachos2005-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sea_level">Sea level</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Paleocene%E2%80%93Eocene_Thermal_Maximum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Sea level">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Past_sea_level#Changes_through_geologic_time" title="Past sea level">Past sea level § Changes through geologic time</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sea_level_rise" title="Sea level rise">Sea level rise</a></div>
<p>Despite the global lack of ice, the sea level would have risen due to thermal expansion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sluijs2006_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sluijs2006-18">[18]</a></sup>
Evidence for this can be found in the shifting <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Palynomorph" title="Palynomorph">palynomorph</a> assemblages of the Arctic Ocean, which reflect a relative decrease in terrestrial organic material compared to marine organic matter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sluijs2006_18-2"><a href="#cite_note-Sluijs2006-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Currents">Currents</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Paleocene%E2%80%93Eocene_Thermal_Maximum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Currents">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>At the start of the PETM, the ocean circulation patterns changed radically in the course of under 5,000 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nunes2006_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nunes2006-32">[32]</a></sup> Global-scale current directions reversed due to a shift in overturning from the southern hemisphere to northern hemisphere overturning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nunes2006_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nunes2006-32">[32]</a></sup>  This "backwards" flow persisted for 40,000 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nunes2006_32-2"><a href="#cite_note-Nunes2006-32">[32]</a></sup> Such a change would transport warm water to the deep oceans, enhancing further warming.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nunes2006_32-3"><a href="#cite_note-Nunes2006-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Lysocline">Lysocline</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Paleocene%E2%80%93Eocene_Thermal_Maximum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Lysocline">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Lysocline" title="Lysocline">lysocline</a> marks the depth at which carbonate starts to dissolve (above the lysocline, carbonate is oversaturated): today, this is at about 4 km, comparable to the median depth of the oceans. This depth depends on (among other things) temperature and the amount of CO<sub>2</sub> dissolved in the ocean. Adding CO<sub>2</sub> initially raises the lysocline,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dickens2_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dickens2-33">[33]</a></sup> resulting in the dissolution of deep water carbonates. This deep-water acidification can be observed in ocean cores, which show (where <a href="/wiki/Bioturbation" title="Bioturbation">bioturbation</a> has not destroyed the signal) an abrupt change from grey carbonate ooze to red clays (followed by a gradual grading back to grey). It is far more pronounced in north Atlantic cores than elsewhere, suggesting that acidification was more concentrated here, related to a greater rise in the level of the lysocline. In parts of the southeast Atlantic, the lysocline rose by 2 km in just a few thousand years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zachos2005_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-Zachos2005-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Life">Life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Paleocene%E2%80%93Eocene_Thermal_Maximum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Stoichiometric <a href="/wiki/Magnetite" title="Magnetite">magnetite</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">Fe<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span>) particles were obtained from PETM-age marine sediments. The study from 2008 found elongate prism and spearhead crystal morphologies, considered unlike any magnetite crystals previously reported, and are potentially of biogenic origin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> These biogenic magnetite crystals show unique gigantism, and probably are of aquatic origin. The study suggests that development of a thick suboxic zones with high iron bioavailability, the result of dramatic changes in weathering and sedimentation rates, drove diversification of magnetite-forming organisms, likely including eukaryotes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> Biogenic magnetite is also found in human brain tissues. Biogenic magnetites in animals have a crucial role in geomagnetic field navigation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ocean_2">Ocean</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Paleocene%E2%80%93Eocene_Thermal_Maximum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Ocean">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The PETM is accompanied by a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mass_extinction" title="Mass extinction">mass extinction</a> of 35-50% of <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/benthic" title="wikt:benthic">benthic</a> <a href="/wiki/Foraminifera" title="Foraminifera">foraminifera</a> (especially in deeper waters) over the course of ~1,000 years – the group suffering more than during the dinosaur-slaying <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/K-T_extinction" title="K-T extinction">K-T extinction</a> (e.g.,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup>). Contrarily, planktonic foraminifera diversified, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dinoflagellates" title="Dinoflagellates">dinoflagellates</a> bloomed. Success was also enjoyed by the <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammals</a>, who radiated extensively around this time.
</p><p>The deep-sea extinctions are difficult to explain, because many species of benthic foraminifera in the deep-sea are cosmopolitan, and can find refugia against local extinction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> General hypotheses such as a temperature-related reduction in oxygen availability, or increased corrosion due to carbonate undersaturated deep waters, are insufficient as explanations. Acidification may also have played a role in the extinction of the calcifying foraminifera,  and the higher temperatures would have increased metabolic rates, thus demanding a higher food supply. Such a higher food supply might not have materialized because warming and increased ocean stratification might have led to declining productivity <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> and/or increased remineralization of organic matter in the water column, before it reached the benthic foraminifera on the sea floor (<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup>). The only factor global in extent was an increase in temperature. Regional extinctions in the North Atlantic can be attributed to increased deep-sea anoxia, which could be due to the slowdown of overturning ocean currents,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Panchuk2008_21-2"><a href="#cite_note-Panchuk2008-21">[21]</a></sup> or the release and rapid oxidation of large amounts of methane. Oxygen minimum zones in the oceans may have expanded.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p><p>In shallower waters, it's undeniable that increased CO<sub>2</sub> levels result in a decreased oceanic <a href="/wiki/PH" title="PH">pH</a>, which has a profound negative effect on corals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Langdon2000_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Langdon2000-44">[44]</a></sup> Experiments suggest it is also very harmful to calcifying plankton.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Riebesell2000_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Riebesell2000-45">[45]</a></sup> However, the strong acids used to simulate the natural increase in acidity which would result from elevated CO<sub>2</sub> concentrations may have given misleading results, and the most recent evidence is that <a href="/wiki/Coccolithophore" title="Coccolithophore">coccolithophores</a> (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/E._huxleyi" title="E. huxleyi">E. huxleyi</a></i> at least) become <i>more</i>, not less, calcified and abundant in acidic waters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Iglesias2008_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Iglesias2008-46">[46]</a></sup> No change in the distribution of calcareous nanoplankton such as the coccolithophores can be attributed to acidification during the PETM.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Iglesias2008_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-Iglesias2008-46">[46]</a></sup> Acidification did lead to an abundance of heavily calcified algae<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bralower2002_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bralower2002-47">[47]</a></sup> and weakly calcified forams.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kelly1998_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kelly1998-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Land">Land</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Paleocene%E2%80%93Eocene_Thermal_Maximum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Land">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Humid conditions caused migration of modern Asian mammals northward, dependent on the climatic belts. Uncertainty remains for the timing and tempo of migration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Adatte2014_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Adatte2014-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p><p>The increase in mammalian abundance is intriguing.  Increased CO<sub>2</sub> levels may have promoted dwarfing<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gingerich2003_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gingerich2003-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Secord2012_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Secord2012-51">[51]</a></sup> – which may have encouraged speciation. Many major mammalian orders – including the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Artiodactyla" title="Artiodactyla">Artiodactyla</a>, horses, and primates – appeared and spread around the globe 13,000 to 22,000 years after the initiation of the PETM.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gingerich2003_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gingerich2003-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Temperature">Temperature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Paleocene%E2%80%93Eocene_Thermal_Maximum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Temperature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Proxy data from one of the studied sites show rapid +8 °C temperature rise, in accordance with existing regional records of marine and terrestrial environments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Adatte2014_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-Adatte2014-49">[49]</a></sup> Notable is the absence of documented greater warming in polar regions. This implies a non existing ice-albedo feedback, hence suggest no sea or land ice was present in the late Paleocene.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bowen2015_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bowen2015-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Terrestrial">Terrestrial</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Paleocene%E2%80%93Eocene_Thermal_Maximum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Terrestrial">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>During the PETM, sediments are enriched with <a href="/wiki/Kaolinite" title="Kaolinite">kaolinite</a> from a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Detrital" title="Detrital">detrital</a> source due to  <a href="/wiki/Denudation" title="Denudation">denudation</a> (initial processes such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Volcanoes" title="Volcanoes">volcanoes</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earthquakes" title="Earthquakes">earthquakes</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Plate_tectonics" title="Plate tectonics">plate tectonics</a>). This suggests increased precipitation, and enhanced erosion of older kaolinite-rich soils and sediments. Increased weathering from the enhanced runoff formed thick paleosoil enriched with <a href="/wiki/Concretion" title="Concretion">carbonate nodules</a> (<a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Microcodium&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Microcodium (page does not exist)">Microcodium</a> like), and this suggests a <a href="/wiki/Semi-arid_climate" title="Semi-arid climate">semi-arid climate</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Adatte2014_49-2"><a href="#cite_note-Adatte2014-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Possible_causes">Possible causes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Paleocene%E2%80%93Eocene_Thermal_Maximum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Possible causes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Discriminating between different possible causes of the PETM is difficult. Temperatures were rising globally at a steady pace, and a mechanism must be invoked to produce an instantaneous spike which may have been accentuated by positive feedbacks. The biggest aid in disentangling these factors comes from a consideration of the carbon isotope mass balance. We know the entire <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/exogenic" title="wikt:exogenic">exogenic</a> <a href="/wiki/Carbon_cycle" title="Carbon cycle">carbon cycle</a> (i.e. the carbon contained within the oceans and atmosphere, which can change on short timescales) underwent a −0.2 % to −0.3 % perturbation in <span class="texhtml">δ</span><sup>13</sup>C<sub></sub>, and by considering the isotopic signatures of other carbon reserves, can consider what mass of the reserve would be necessary to produce this effect. The assumption underpinning this approach is that the mass of exogenic carbon was the same in the <a href="/wiki/Paleogene" title="Paleogene">Paleogene</a> as it is today – something which is very difficult to confirm.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Eruption_of_large_kimberlite_field">Eruption of large kimberlite field</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Paleocene%E2%80%93Eocene_Thermal_Maximum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Eruption of large kimberlite field">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Although the cause of the initial warming has been attributed to a massive injection of carbon (CO<sub>2</sub> and/or CH<sub>4</sub>) into the atmosphere, the source of the carbon has yet to be found. The emplacement of a large cluster of <a href="/wiki/Kimberlite" title="Kimberlite">kimberlite</a> pipes at ~56 Ma in the <a href="/wiki/Lac_de_Gras" title="Lac de Gras">Lac de Gras</a> region of northern Canada may have provided the carbon that triggered early warming in the form of exsolved magmatic CO<sub>2</sub>. Calculations indicate that the estimated 900-1,100 Pg<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> of carbon required for the initial ~3 °C of ocean water warming associated with the Paleocene-Eocene thermal maximum could have been released during the emplacement of a large kimberlite cluster.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> The transfer of warm surface ocean water to intermediate depths led to thermal dissociation of seafloor methane hydrates, providing the isotopically depleted carbon that produced the carbon isotopic excursion. The coeval ages of two other kimberlite clusters in the Lac de Gras field and two other early Cenozoic hyperthermals indicate that CO<sub>2</sub> degassing during kimberlite emplacement is a plausible source of the CO<sub>2</sub> responsible for these sudden global warming events.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Volcanic_activity">Volcanic activity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Paleocene%E2%80%93Eocene_Thermal_Maximum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Volcanic activity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wfm_ardnamurchan_landsat.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="948" data-file-width="1280" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Wfm_ardnamurchan_landsat.jpg/220px-Wfm_ardnamurchan_landsat.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Wfm_ardnamurchan_landsat.jpg/330px-Wfm_ardnamurchan_landsat.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Wfm_ardnamurchan_landsat.jpg/440px-Wfm_ardnamurchan_landsat.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wfm_ardnamurchan_landsat.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Satellite photo of <a href="/wiki/Ardnamurchan" title="Ardnamurchan">Ardnamurchan</a> – with clearly visible circular shape, which is the 'plumbings of an ancient volcano'</div></div></div>
<p>To balance the mass of carbon and produce the observed <span class="texhtml">δ</span><sup>13</sup>C<sub></sub> value, at least 1,500 gigatons of carbon would have to degas from the mantle via volcanoes over the course of the two, 1,000 year, steps. To put this in perspective, this is about 200 times the background rate of degassing for the rest of the Paleocene. There is no indication that such a burst of volcanic activity has occurred at any point in Earth's history. However, substantial volcanism had been active in East Greenland for around the preceding million years or so, but this struggles to explain the rapidity of the PETM. Even if the bulk of the 1,500 gigatons of carbon was released in a single pulse, further feedbacks would be necessary to produce the observed isotopic excursion.
</p><p>On the other hand, there are suggestions that surges of activity occurred in the later stages of the volcanism and associated continental rifting. Intrusions of hot magma into carbon-rich sediments may have triggered the degassing of isotopically light methane in sufficient volumes to cause global warming and the observed isotope anomaly. This hypothesis is documented by the presence of extensive intrusive sill complexes and thousands of kilometer-sized hydrothermal vent complexes in sedimentary basins on the mid-Norwegian margin and west of Shetland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Svensen2004_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Svensen2004-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Storey2007_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Storey2007-55">[55]</a></sup>  Volcanic eruptions of a large magnitude can impact global climate, reducing the amount of solar radiation reaching the Earth's surface, lowering temperatures in the troposphere, and changing atmospheric circulation patterns.  Large-scale volcanic activity may last only a few days, but the massive outpouring of gases and ash can influence climate patterns for years.  Sulfuric gases convert to sulfate aerosols, sub-micron droplets containing about 75 percent sulfuric acid. Following eruptions, these aerosol particles can linger as long as three to four years in the stratosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wolfe2000_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wolfe2000-56">[56]</a></sup>  
Further phases of volcanic activity could have triggered the release of more methane, and caused other early Eocene warm events such as the <a href="/wiki/Eocene_Thermal_Maximum_2" title="Eocene Thermal Maximum 2">ETM2</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Panchuk2008_21-3"><a href="#cite_note-Panchuk2008-21">[21]</a></sup>  
It has also been suggested that volcanic activity around the Caribbean may have disrupted the circulation of oceanic currents,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bralower1997_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bralower1997-57">[57]</a></sup> amplifying the magnitude of climate change.
</p><p>A 2017 study noted strong evidence of a volcanic carbon source (greater than 10,000 petagrams), associated with the <a href="/wiki/North_Atlantic_Igneous_Province" title="North Atlantic Igneous Province">North Atlantic Igneous Province</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ArsAug17_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-ArsAug17-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Comet_impact">Comet impact</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Paleocene%E2%80%93Eocene_Thermal_Maximum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Comet impact">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A briefly popular theory held that a <sup>12</sup>C-rich comet struck the earth and initiated the warming event. A cometary impact coincident with the P/E boundary can also help explain some enigmatic features associated with this event, such as the iridium anomaly at <a href="/wiki/Zumaia" title="Zumaia">Zumaia</a>, the abrupt appearance of kaolinitic clays with abundant magnetic nanoparticles on the coastal shelf of New Jersey, and especially the nearly simultaneous onset of the carbon isotope excursion and the thermal maximum. Indeed, a key feature and testable prediction of a comet impact is that it should produce virtually instantaneous environmental effects in the atmosphere and surface ocean with later repercussions in the deeper ocean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kent2003_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kent2003-59">[59]</a></sup> Even allowing for feedback processes, this would require at least 100 gigatons of extraterrestrial carbon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kent2003_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kent2003-59">[59]</a></sup> Such a catastrophic impact should have left its mark on the globe. Unfortunately, the evidence put forward does not stand up to scrutiny. An unusual 9-meter-thick clay layer supposedly formed soon after the impact, containing unusual amounts of magnetite, but it formed too slowly for these magnetic particles to have been a result of the comet's impact.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sluijs2007_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sluijs2007-29">[29]</a></sup> and it turns out they were created by bacteria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kopp2007_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kopp2007-60">[60]</a></sup>  However, recent analyses have shown that isolated particles of non-biogenic origin make up the majority of the magnetic particles in the thick clay unit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wang2012_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wang2012-61">[61]</a></sup>
</p><p>A 2016 report in <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Science_Magazine" title="Science Magazine">Science</a></i> describes the discovery of impact ejecta from three marine P-E boundary sections from the Atlantic margin of the eastern U.S., indicating that an extraterrestrial impact occurred during the carbon isotope excursion at the P-E boundary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SchallerFung2016_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-SchallerFung2016-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> The silicate glass spherules found were identified as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microtektite" title="Microtektite">microtektites</a> and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Microkrystite&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Microkrystite (page does not exist)">microkrystites</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SchallerFung2016_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-SchallerFung2016-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Burning_of_peat">Burning of peat</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Paleocene%E2%80%93Eocene_Thermal_Maximum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Burning of peat">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The combustion of prodigious quantities of <a href="/wiki/Peat" title="Peat">peat</a> was once postulated, because there was probably a greater mass of carbon stored as living terrestrial biomass during the Paleocene than there is today since plants in fact grew more vigorously during the period of the PETM. This theory was refuted, because in order to produce the <span class="texhtml">δ</span><sup>13</sup>C<sub></sub> excursion observed, over 90% of the Earth's biomass would have to have been combusted.  However, the Paleocene is also recognized as a time of significant peat accumulation worldwide. A comprehensive search failed to find evidence for the combustion of fossil organic matter, in the form of soot or similar particulate carbon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Orbital_forcing">Orbital forcing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Paleocene%E2%80%93Eocene_Thermal_Maximum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Orbital forcing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The presence of later (smaller) warming events of a global scale, such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Elmo_horizon" title="Elmo horizon">Elmo horizon</a> (aka <a href="/wiki/Eocene_Thermal_Maximum_2" title="Eocene Thermal Maximum 2">ETM2</a>), has led to the hypothesis that the events repeat on a regular basis, driven by maxima in the 400,000 and 100,000 year eccentricity <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Milankovic_cycles" title="Milankovic cycles">cycles</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_orbit" title="Earth's orbit">Earth's orbit</a>. The current warming period is expected to last another 50,000 years due to a minimum in the eccentricity of the Earth's orbit. Orbital increase in insolation (and thus temperature) would force the system over a threshold and unleash positive feedbacks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lourens2005_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lourens2005-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Methane_release">Methane release</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Paleocene%E2%80%93Eocene_Thermal_Maximum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Methane release">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ambox_question.svg"><img alt="Ambox question.svg" data-file-height="40" data-file-width="40" height="40" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Ambox_question.svg/40px-Ambox_question.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Ambox_question.svg/60px-Ambox_question.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Ambox_question.svg/80px-Ambox_question.svg.png 2x" width="40"/></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This article or section <b>possibly contains <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:No_original_research#Synthesis_of_published_material" title="Wikipedia:No original research">synthesis of material</a> which does not <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verifiably mention</a> or <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Content_removal#Irrelevant_information" title="Wikipedia:Content removal">relate</a> to the main topic.</b><span class="hide-when-compact"> Relevant discussion may be found on the <a href="/wiki/Talk:Paleocene%E2%80%93Eocene_Thermal_Maximum##" title="Talk:Paleocene–Eocene Thermal Maximum">talk page</a>.</span> <small><i>(January 2016)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>None of the above causes are alone sufficient to cause the carbon isotope excursion or warming observed at the PETM. The most obvious feedback mechanism that could amplify the initial perturbation is that of <a href="/wiki/Methane_clathrate" title="Methane clathrate">methane clathrates</a>. Under certain temperature and pressure conditions, methane – which is being produced continually by decomposing microbes in sea bottom sediments – is stable in a complex with water, which forms ice-like cages trapping the methane in solid form. As temperature rises, the pressure required to keep this clathrate configuration stable increases, so shallow clathrates dissociate, releasing methane gas to make its way into the atmosphere. Since biogenic clathrates have a <span class="texhtml">δ</span><sup>13</sup>C<sub></sub> signature of −60 ‰ (inorganic clathrates are the still rather large −40 ‰), relatively small masses can produce large <span class="texhtml">δ</span><sup>13</sup>C<sub></sub> excursions. Further, methane is a potent <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gas</a> as it is released into the atmosphere, so it causes warming, and as the ocean transports this warmth to the bottom sediments, it destabilizes more clathrates. It would take around 2,300 years for an increased temperature to diffuse warmth into the sea bed to a depth sufficient to cause a release of clathrates, although the exact time-frame is highly dependent on a number of poorly constrained assumptions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Katz2001_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-Katz2001-66">[66]</a></sup> Ocean warming due to flooding and pressure changes due to a sea-level drop may have caused clathrates to become unstable and release methane. This can take place over as short of a period as a few thousand years. The reverse process, that of fixing methane in clathrates, occurs over a larger scale of tens of thousands of years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p><p>In order for the clathrate hypothesis to work, the oceans must show signs of having been warmer slightly before the carbon isotope excursion, because it would take some time for the methane to become mixed into the system and <span class="texhtml">δ</span><sup>13</sup>C<sub></sub>-reduced carbon to be returned to the deep ocean sedimentary record. Until recently, the evidence suggested that the two peaks were in fact simultaneous, weakening the support for the methane theory. But recent (2002) work has managed to detect a short gap between the initial warming and the <span class="texhtml">δ</span><sup>13</sup>C<sub></sub> excursion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thomas2002_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas2002-68">[68]</a></sup>  Chemical markers of surface temperature (<a href="/wiki/TEX86" title="TEX86">TEX<sub>86</sub></a>) also indicate that warming occurred around 3,000 years before the carbon isotope excursion, but this does not seem to hold true for all cores.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sluijs2007_29-2"><a href="#cite_note-Sluijs2007-29">[29]</a></sup>  Notably, deeper (non-surface) waters do not appear to display evidence of this time gap.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tripati2005_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tripati2005-69">[69]</a></sup>  Moreover, the small apparent change in TEX<sub>86</sub> that precede the <span class="texhtml">δ</span><sup>13</sup>C<sub></sub> anomaly can easily (and more plausibly) be ascribed to local variability (especially on the Atlantic coastal plain, e.g. Sluijs, et al., 2007) as the TEX<sub>86</sub> paleo-thermometer is prone to significant biological effects.  The <span class="texhtml">δ</span><sup>18</sup>O<sub></sub> of benthic or planktonic forams does not show any pre-warming in any of these localities, and in an ice-free world, it is generally a much more reliable indicator of past ocean temperatures.
</p><p>Analysis of these records reveals another interesting fact: planktonic (floating) forams record the shift to lighter isotope values earlier than benthic (bottom dwelling) forams. The lighter (lower <span class="texhtml">δ</span><sup>13</sup>C<sub></sub>) methanogenic carbon can only be incorporated into the forams' shells after it has been oxidised. A gradual release of the gas would allow it to be oxidised in the deep ocean, which would make benthic forams show lighter values earlier. The fact that the planktonic forams are the first to show the signal suggests that the methane was released so rapidly that its oxidation used up all the oxygen at depth in the water column, allowing some methane to reach the atmosphere unoxidised, where atmospheric oxygen would react with it. This observation also allows us to constrain the duration of methane release to under around 10,000 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thomas2002_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas2002-68">[68]</a></sup>
</p><p>However, there are several major problems with the methane hydrate dissociation hypothesis. The most parsimonious interpretation for surface-water forams to show the <span class="texhtml">δ</span><sup>13</sup>C<sub></sub> excursion before their benthic counterparts (as in the Thomas et al. paper) is that the perturbation occurred from the top down, and not the bottom up.  If the anomalous <span class="texhtml">δ</span><sup>13</sup>C<sub></sub> (in whatever form: CH<sub>4</sub> or CO<sub>2</sub>) entered the atmospheric carbon reservoir first, and then diffused into the surface ocean waters, which mix with the deeper ocean waters over much longer time-scales, we would expect to observe the planktonics shifting toward lighter values before the benthics.  Moreover, careful examination of the Thomas et al. data set shows that there is not a single intermediate planktonic foram value, implying that the perturbation and attendant <span class="texhtml">δ</span><sup>13</sup>C<sub></sub> anomaly happened over the lifespan of a single foram – much too fast for the nominal 10,000-year release needed for the methane hypothesis to work.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>There is a debate about whether there was a large enough amount of methane hydrate to be a major carbon source; a recent paper proposed that was the case.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gu2011_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gu2011-70">[70]</a></sup> The present-day global methane hydrate reserve is poorly constrained, but is mostly considered to be between 2,000 and 10,000 Gt. However, because the global ocean bottom temperatures were ~6 °C higher than today, which implies a much smaller volume of sediment hosting gas hydrate than today, the global amount of hydrate before the PETM has been thought to be much less than present-day estimates. in a 2006 study, scientists regarded the source of carbon for the PETM to be a mystery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pagani_Science_2006_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pagani_Science_2006-71">[71]</a></sup>  A 2011 study, using numerical simulations suggests that enhanced organic carbon <a href="/wiki/Sedimentation" title="Sedimentation">sedimentation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Methanogenesis" title="Methanogenesis">methanogenesis</a> could have compensated for the smaller volume of hydrate stability.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gu2011_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gu2011-70">[70]</a></sup>
</p><p>A 2016 study based on reconstructions of atmospheric CO<sub>2</sub> content during the PETM's carbon isotope excursions (CIE), using <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Triple_oxygen_isotope_analysis&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Triple oxygen isotope analysis (page does not exist)">triple oxygen isotope analysis</a>, suggests a massive release of seabed methane into the atmosphere, and the driver of climatic changes. The authors also note:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>A massive release of methane clathrates by thermal dissociation has been the most convincing hypothesis to explain the CIE since it was first identified.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ocean_circulation">Ocean circulation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Paleocene%E2%80%93Eocene_Thermal_Maximum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Ocean circulation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The large scale patterns of ocean circulation are important when considering how heat was transported through the oceans. Our understanding of these patterns is still in a preliminary stage. Models show that there are possible mechanisms to quickly transport heat to the shallow, clathrate-containing ocean shelves, given the right bathymetric profile, but the models cannot yet match the distribution of data we observe.  "Warming accompanying a south-to-north switch in deepwater formation would produce sufficient warming to destabilize seafloor gas hydrates over most of the world ocean to a water depth of at least 1900 m."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bice2002_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bice2002-73">[73]</a></sup> This destabilization could have resulted in the release of more than 2000 gigatons of methane gas from the clathrate zone of the ocean floor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bice2002_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bice2002-73">[73]</a></sup>
</p><p>Arctic freshwater input into the North Pacific could serve as a catalyst for methane hydrate destabilization, an event suggested as a precursor to the onset of the PETM.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cope,_Jesse_Tiner_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cope,_Jesse_Tiner-74">[74]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Recovery">Recovery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Paleocene%E2%80%93Eocene_Thermal_Maximum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Recovery">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><a href="/wiki/Proxy_(climate)" title="Proxy (climate)">Climate proxies</a>, such as ocean sediments (depositional rates) indicate a duration of ∼83 ka, with ∼33 ka
in the early rapid phase and ∼50 ka in a subsequent gradual phase.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McInerney2011_3-7"><a href="#cite_note-McInerney2011-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>The most likely method of recovery involves an increase in biological productivity, transporting carbon to the deep ocean. This would be assisted by higher global temperatures and CO<sub>2</sub> levels, as well as an increased nutrient supply (which would result from higher continental weathering due to higher temperatures and rainfall; volcanoes may have provided further nutrients). Evidence for higher biological productivity comes in the form of bio-concentrated <a href="/wiki/Barium" title="Barium">barium</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bains2000_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bains2000-75">[75]</a></sup> However, this proxy may instead reflect the addition of barium dissolved in methane.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dickens2003_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dickens2003-76">[76]</a></sup> Diversifications suggest that productivity increased in near-shore environments, which would have been warm and fertilized by run-off, outweighing the reduction in productivity in the deep oceans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kelly1998_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kelly1998-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Paleocene%E2%80%93Eocene_Thermal_Maximum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
